          Coulomb\n             </a>\n            </li>\n            <li>\n             <a href="/wiki/Michael_Faraday" title="Michael Faraday">\n              Faraday\n             </a>\n            </li>\n            <li>\n             <a href="/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss" title="Carl Friedrich Gauss">\n              Gauss\n             </a>\n            </li>\n            <li>\n             <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Heaviside" title="Oliver Heaviside">\n              Heaviside\n             </a>\n            </li>\n            <li>\n             <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Henry" title="Joseph Henry">\n              Henry\n             </a>\n            </li>\n            <li>\n             <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Hertz" title="Heinrich Hertz">\n              Hertz\n             </a>\n            </li>\n            <li>\n             <a href="/wiki/Hendrik_Lorentz" title="Hendrik Lorentz">\n              Lorentz\n             </a>\n            </li>\n            <li>\n             <a href="/wiki/James_Clerk_Maxwell" title="James Clerk Maxwell">\n              Maxwell\n             </a>\n            </li>\n            <li>\n             <a href="/wiki/Nikola_Tesla" title="Nikola Tesla">\n              Tesla\n             </a>\n            </li>\n            <li>\n             <a href="/wiki/Alessandro_Volta" title="Alessandro Volta">\n              Volta\n             </a>\n            </li>\n            <li>\n             <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_Eduard_Weber" title="Wilhelm Eduard Weber">\n              Weber\n             </a>\n            </li>\n            <li>\n             <a href="/wiki/Hans_Christian_%C3%98rsted" title="Hans Christian \xc3\x98rsted">\n              \xc3\x98rsted\n             </a>\n            </li>\n           </ul>\n          </div>\n         </div>\n        </td>\n       </tr>\n       <tr>\n        <td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;">\n         <div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini">\n          <ul>\n           <li class="nv-view">\n            <a href="/wiki/Template:Electromagnetism" title="Template:Electromagnetism">\n             <abbr title="View this template">\n              v\n             </abbr>\n            </a>\n           </li>\n           <li class="nv-talk">\n            <a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Electromagnetism" title="Template talk:Electromagnetism">\n             <abbr title="Discuss this template">\n              t\n             </abbr>\n            </a>\n           </li>\n           <li class="nv-edit">\n            <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Electromagnetism&amp;action=edit">\n             <abbr title="Edit this template">\n              e\n             </abbr>\n            </a>\n           </li>\n          </ul>\n         </div>\n        </td>\n       </tr>\n      </table>\n      <p>\n       <b>\n        Electricity\n       </b>\n       is the set of\n       <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">\n        physical\n       </a>\n       phenomena associated with the presence and motion of\n       <a href="/wiki/Electric_charge" title="Electric charge">\n        electric charge\n       </a>\n       . Although initially considered a phenomenon separate from\n       <a href="/wiki/Magnetism" title="Magnetism">\n        magnetism\n       </a>\n       , since the development of\n       <a href="/wiki/Maxwell%27s_equations" title="Maxwell\'s equations">\n        Maxwell\'s equations\n       </a>\n       , both are recognized as part of a single phenomenon:\n       <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetism" title="Electromagnetism">\n        electromagnetism\n       </a>\n       . Various common phenomena are related to electricity, including\n       <a href="/wiki/Lightning" title="Lightning">\n        lightning\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Static_electricity" title="Static electricity">\n        static electricity\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Electric_heating" title="Electric heating">\n        electric heating\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Electric_discharge" title="Electric discharge">\n        electric discharges\n       </a>\n       and many others.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The presence of an electric charge, which can be either positive or negative, produces an\n       <a href="/wiki/Electric_field" title="Electric field">\n        electric field\n       </a>\n       . On the other hand, the movement of electric charges, which is known as\n       <a href="/wiki/Electric_current" title="Electric current">\n        electric current\n       </a>\n       , produces a\n       <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">\n        magnetic field\n       </a>\n       .\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       When a charge is placed in a location with a non-zero electric field, a force will act on it. The magnitude of this force is given by\n       <a href="/wiki/Coulomb%27s_law" title="Coulomb\'s law">\n        Coulomb\'s law\n       </a>\n       . Thus, if that charge were to move, the electric field would be doing\n       <a href="/wiki/Work_(physics)" title="Work (physics)">\n        work\n       </a>\n       on the electric charge. Thus we can speak of\n       <a href="/wiki/Electric_potential" title="Electric potential">\n        electric potential\n       </a>\n       at a certain point in space, which is equal to the work done by an external agent in carrying a unit of positive charge from an arbitrarily chosen reference point to that point without any acceleration and is typically measured in\n       <a href="/wiki/Volt" title="Volt">\n        volts\n       </a>\n       .\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Electricity is at the heart of many modern technologies, being used for:\n      </p>\n      <ul>\n       <li>\n        <b>\n         <a href="/wiki/Electric_power" title="Electric power">\n          electric power\n         </a>\n        </b>\n        where electric current is used to energise equipment;\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        <b>\n         <a href="/wiki/Electronics" title="Electronics">\n          electronics\n         </a>\n        </b>\n        which deals with\n        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_circuit" title="Electrical circuit">\n         electrical circuits\n        </a>\n        that involve\n        <a href="/wiki/Passivity_(engineering)" title="Passivity (engineering)">\n         active electrical components\n        </a>\n        such as\n        <a href="/wiki/Vacuum_tube" title="Vacuum tube">\n         vacuum tubes\n        </a>\n        ,\n        <a href="/wiki/Transistor" title="Transistor">\n         transistors\n        </a>\n        ,\n        <a href="/wiki/Diode" title="Diode">\n         diodes\n        </a>\n        and\n        <a href="/wiki/Integrated_circuit" title="Integrated circuit">\n         integrated circuits\n        </a>\n        , and associated passive interconnection technologies.\n       </li>\n      </ul>\n      <p>\n       Electrical phenomena have been studied since antiquity, though progress in theoretical understanding remained slow until the seventeenth and eighteenth centuries. Even then, practical applications for electricity were few, and it would not be until the late nineteenth century that\n       <a href="/wiki/Electrical_engineering" title="Electrical engineering">\n        electrical engineers\n       </a>\n       were able to put it to industrial and residential use. The rapid expansion in electrical technology at this time transformed industry and society, becoming a driving force for the\n       <a href="/wiki/Second_Industrial_Revolution" title="Second Industrial Revolution">\n        Second Industrial Revolution\n       </a>\n       . Electricity\'s extraordinary versatility means it can be put to an almost limitless set of applications which include\n       <a href="/wiki/Motive_power" title="Motive power">\n        transport\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/HVAC" title="HVAC">\n        heating\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Electric_light" title="Electric light">\n        lighting\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Telecommunication" title="Telecommunication">\n        communications\n       </a>\n       , and\n       <a href="/wiki/Computation" title="Computation">\n        computation\n       </a>\n       . Electrical power is now the backbone of modern industrial society.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-1">\n         [1]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n      </p>\n      <div class="toc" id="toc">\n       <div class="toctitle">\n        <h2>\n         Contents\n        </h2>\n       </div>\n       <ul>\n        <li class="toclevel-1 potential\n            </span>\n           </a>\n          </li>\n          <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-7">\n           <a href="#Electromagnets">\n            <span class="tocnumber">\n             2.5\n            </span>\n            <span class="toctext">\n             Electromagnets\n            </span>\n           </a>\n          </li>\n          <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-8">\n           <a href="#Electrochemistry">\n            <span class="tocnumber">\n             2.6\n            </span>\n            <span class="toctext">\n             Electrochemistry\n           <span class="tocnumber">\n             2.10\n            </span>\n            <span class="toctext">\n             Electromagnetic wave\n            </span>\n           </a>\n          </li>\n         </ul>\n        </li>\n        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-13">\n         <a href="#Production_and_uses">\n          <span class="tocnumber">\n           3\n          </span>\n          <span class="toctext">\n           Production and uses\n          </span>\n         </a>\n         <ul>\n          <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-14">\n           <a href="#Generation_and_transmission">\n            <span class="tocnumber">\n             3.1\n            </span>\n            <span class="toctext">\n             Generation and transmission\n            </span>\n           </a>\n          </li>\n          <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-15">\n           <a href="#Applications">\n            <span class="tocnumber">\n             3.2\n            </span>\n            <span class="toctext">\n             Applications\n            </span>\n           </a>\n          </li>\n         </ul>\n        </li>\n        <li class="toclevel-1 and the natural world\n          </span>\n         </a>\n         <ul>\n          <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-17">\n           <a href="#Physiological_effects">\n            <span class="tocnumber">\n             4.1\n            </span>\n            <span class="toctext">\n             Physiological effects\n            </span>\n           </a>\n          </li>\n          <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-18">\n           <a href="#Electrical_phenomena_in_nature">\n            <span class="tocnumber">\n             4.2\n            </span>\n            <span class="toctext">\n             Electrical phenomena in nature\n            </span>\n           </a>\n          </li>\n         </ul>\n        </li>\n        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-19">\n         <a href="#Cultural_perception">\n          <span class="tocnumber">\n           5\n          </span>\n          <span class="toctext">\n           Cultural perception\n          </span>\n         </a>\n        </li>\n        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-20">\n         <a href="#See_also">\n          <span class="tocnumber">\n           6\n          </span>\n          <span class="toctext">\n           See also\n          </span>\n         </a>\n        </li>\n        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-21">\n         <a href="#Notes">\n          <span class="tocnumber">\n           7\n          </span>\n          <span class="toctext">\n           Notes\n          </span>\n         </a>\n        </li>\n        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-22">\n         <a href="#References">\n          <span class="tocnumber">\n           8\n          </span>\n          <span class="toctext">\n           References\n          </span>\n         </a>\n        </li>\n        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-23">\n         <a href="#External_links">\n          <span class="tocnumber">\n           9\n          </span>\n          <span class="toctext">\n           External links\n          </span>\n         </a>\n        </li>\n       </ul>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n      </p>\n      <h2>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="History">\n        History\n       </span>\n      </h2>\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Thales.jpg">\n         <img alt="A bust of a bearded man with dishevelled hair" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="463" data-file-width="361" height="218" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Thales.jpg/170px-Thales.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Thales.jpg/255px-Thales.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Thales.jpg/340px-Thales.jpg 2x" width="170"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Thales.jpg" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thales" title="Thales">\n          Thales\n         </a>\n         , the earliest known researcher into electricity\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       Main articles:\n       <a href="/wiki/History_of_electromagnetic_theory" title="History of electromagnetic theory">\n        History of electromagnetic theory\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/History_of_electrical_engineering" title="History of electrical engineering">\n        History of electrical engineering\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       See also:\n       <a href="/wiki/Etymology_of_electricity" title="Etymology of electricity">\n        Etymology of electricity\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       Long before any knowledge of electricity existed, people were aware of shocks from\n       <a href="/wiki/Electric_fish" title="Electric fish">\n        electric fish\n       </a>\n       .\n       <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">\n        Ancient Egyptian\n       </a>\n       texts dating from\n       <a href="/wiki/28th_century_BC" title="28th century BC">\n        2750 BCE\n       </a>\n       referred to these fish as the "Thunderer of the\n       <a href="/wiki/Nile" title="Nile">\n        Nile\n       </a>\n       ", and described them as the "protectors" of all other fish. Electric fish were again reported millennia later by\n       <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">\n        ancient Greek\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">\n        Roman\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_geography" title="Islamic geography">\n        Arabic naturalists\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_medicine" title="Islamic medicine">\n        physicians\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2">\n        <a href="#cite_note-2">\n         [2]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Several ancient writers, such as\n       <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">\n        Pliny the Elder\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/Scribonius_Largus" title="Scribonius Largus">\n        Scribonius Largus\n       </a>\n       , attested to the numbing effect of\n       <a href="/wiki/Electric_shock" title="Electric shock">\n        electric shocks\n       </a>\n       delivered by\n       <a href="/wiki/Electric_catfish" title="Electric catfish">\n        catfish\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/Electric_ray" title="Electric ray">\n        electric rays\n       </a>\n       , and knew that such shocks could travel along conducting objects.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Electroreception_3-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Electroreception-3">\n         [3]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Patients suffering from ailments such as\n       <a href="/wiki/Gout" title="Gout">\n        gout\n       </a>\n       or\n       <a href="/wiki/Headache" title="Headache">\n        headache\n       </a>\n       were directed to touch electric fish in the hope that the powerful jolt might cure them.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-morris_4-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-morris-4">\n         [4]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Possibly the earliest and nearest approach to the discovery of the identity of\n       <a href="/wiki/Lightning" title="Lightning">\n        lightning\n       </a>\n       , and electricity from any other source, is to be attributed to the\n       <a href="/wiki/Arabs" title="Arabs">\n        Arabs\n       </a>\n       , who before the 15th century had the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">\n        Arabic\n       </a>\n       word for lightning\n       <i>\n        ra\xe2\x80\x98ad\n       </i>\n       (\n       <span class="Unicode">\n        \xd8\xb1\xd8\xb9\xd8\xaf\n       </span>\n       ) applied to the\n       <a href="/wiki/Electric_ray" title="Electric ray">\n        electric ray\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EncyclopediaAmericana_5-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-EncyclopediaAmericana-5">\n         [5]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Ancient cultures around the\n       <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">\n        Mediterranean\n       </a>\n       knew that certain objects, such as rods of\n       <a href="/wiki/Amber" title="Amber">\n        amber\n       </a>\n       , could be rubbed with cat\'s fur to attract light objects like feathers.\n       <a href="/wiki/Thales_of_Miletus" title="Thales of Miletus">\n        Thales of Miletus\n       </a>\n       made a series of observations on\n       <a href="/wiki/Static_electricity" title="Static electricity">\n        static electricity\n       </a>\n       around 600 BCE, from which he believed that friction rendered amber\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magnetic" title="Magnetic">\n        magnetic\n       </a>\n       , in contrast to minerals such as\n       <a href="/wiki/Magnetite" title="Magnetite">\n        magnetite\n       </a>\n       , which needed no rubbing.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stewart_6-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-stewart-6">\n         [6]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">\n        <a href="#cite_note-7">\n         [7]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">\n        <a href="#cite_note-8">\n         [8]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">\n        <a href="#cite_note-9">\n         [9]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Thales was incorrect in believing the attraction was due to a magnetic effect, but later science would prove a link between magnetism and electricity. According to a controversial theory, the\n       <a href="/wiki/Parthia" title="Parthia">\n        Parthians\n       </a>\n       may have had knowledge of\n       <a href="/wiki/Electroplating" title="Electroplating">\n        electroplating\n       </a>\n       , based on the 1936 discovery of the\n       <a href="/wiki/Baghdad_Battery" title="Baghdad Battery">\n        Baghdad Battery\n       </a>\n       , which resembles a\n       <a href="/wiki/Galvanic_cell" title="Galvanic cell">\n        galvanic cell\n       </a>\n       , though it is uncertain whether the artifact was electrical in nature.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">\n        <a href="#cite_note-10">\n         [10]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <div class="thumb tleft">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Franklin-Benjamin-LOC.jpg">\n         <img alt="A half-length portrait of a bald, somewhat portly man in a three-piece suit." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2866" data-file-width="2129" height="229" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Franklin-Benjamin-LOC.jpg/170px-Franklin-Benjamin-LOC.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Franklin-Benjamin-LOC.jpg/255px-Franklin-Benjamin-LOC.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Franklin-Benjamin-LOC.jpg/340px-Franklin-Benjamin-LOC.jpg 2x" width="170"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Franklin-Benjamin-LOC.jpg" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Franklin" title="Benjamin Franklin">\n          Benjamin Franklin\n         </a>\n         conducted extensive research on electricity in the 18th century, as documented by\n         <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Priestley" title="Joseph Priestley">\n          Joseph Priestley\n         </a>\n         (1767)\n         <i>\n          History and Present Status of Electricity\n         </i>\n         , with whom Franklin carried on extended correspondence.\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       Electricity would remain little more than an intellectual curiosity for millennia until 1600, when the English scientist\n       <a href="/wiki/William_Gilbert_(astronomer)" title="William Gilbert (astronomer)">\n        William Gilbert\n       </a>\n       made a careful study of electricity and magnetism, distinguishing the\n       <a href="/wiki/Lodestone" title="Lodestone">\n        lodestone\n       </a>\n       effect from static electricity produced by rubbing amber.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stewart_6-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-stewart-6">\n         [6]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       He coined the\n       <a href="/wiki/New_Latin" title="New Latin">\n        New Latin\n       </a>\n       word\n       <i>\n        electricus\n       </i>\n       ("of amber" or "like amber", from \xe1\xbc\xa4\xce\xbb\xce\xb5\xce\xba\xcf\x84\xcf\x81\xce\xbf\xce\xbd,\n       <i>\n        elektron\n       </i>\n       , the\n       <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">\n        Greek\n       </a>\n       word for "amber") to refer to the property of attracting small objects after being rubbed.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">\n        <a href="#cite_note-11">\n         [11]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       This association gave rise to the English words "electric" and "electricity", which made their first appearance in print in\n       <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Browne" title="Thomas Browne">\n        Thomas Browne\n       </a>\n       \'s\n       <i>\n        <a href="/wiki/Pseudodoxia_Epidemica" title="Pseudodoxia Epidemica">\n         Pseudodoxia Epidemica\n        </a>\n       </i>\n       of 1646.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">\n        <a href="#cite_note-12">\n         [12]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Further work was conducted by\n       <a href="/wiki/Otto_von_Guericke" title="Otto von Guericke">\n        Otto von Guericke\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Robert_Boyle" title="Robert Boyle">\n        Robert Boyle\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Gray_(scientist)" title="Stephen Gray (scientist)">\n        Stephen Gray\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/C._F._du_Fay" title="C. F. du Fay">\n        C. F. du Fay\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guarnieri_7-1_13-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-guarnieri_7-1-13">\n         [13]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       In the 18th century,\n       <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Franklin" title="Benjamin Franklin">\n        Benjamin Franklin\n       </a>\n       conducted extensive research in electricity, selling his possessions to fund his work. In June 1752 he is reputed to have attached a metal key to the bottom of a dampened kite string and flown the kite in a storm-threatened sky.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">\n        <a href="#cite_note-14">\n         [14]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       A succession of sparks jumping from the key to the back of his hand showed that\n       <a href="/wiki/Lightning" title="Lightning">\n        lightning\n       </a>\n       was indeed electrical in nature.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">\n        <a href="#cite_note-15">\n         [15]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       He also explained the apparently paradoxical behavior\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">\n        <a href="#cite_note-16">\n         [16]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       of the\n       <a href="/wiki/Leyden_jar" title="Leyden jar">\n        Leyden jar\n       </a>\n       as a device for storing large amounts of electrical charge in terms of electricity consisting of both positive and negative charges.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guarnieri_7-1_13-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-guarnieri_7-1-13">\n         [13]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:M_Faraday_Th_Phillips_oil_1842.jpg">\n         <img alt="Half-length portrait oil painting of a man in a dark suit" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1437" data-file-width="1110" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/M_Faraday_Th_Phillips_oil_1842.jpg/170px-M_Faraday_Th_Phillips_oil_1842.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/M_Faraday_Th_Phillips_oil_1842.jpg/255px-M_Faraday_Th_Phillips_oil_1842.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/M_Faraday_Th_Phillips_oil_1842.jpg/340px-M_Faraday_Th_Phillips_oil_1842.jpg 2x" width="170"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:M_Faraday_Th_Phillips_oil_1842.jpg" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         <a href="/wiki/Michael_Faraday" title="Michael Faraday">\n          Michael Faraday\n         </a>\n         \'s discoveries formed the foundation of electric motor technology\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       In 1791,\n       <a href="/wiki/Luigi_Galvani" title="Luigi Galvani">\n        Luigi Galvani\n       </a>\n       published his discovery of\n       <a href="/wiki/Bioelectromagnetics" title="Bioelectromagnetics">\n        bioelectromagnetics\n       </a>\n       , demonstrating that electricity was the medium by which\n       <a href="/wiki/Neuron" title="Neuron">\n        neurons\n       </a>\n       passed signals to the muscles.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guarnieri_7-2_17-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-guarnieri_7-2-17">\n         [17]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kirby_18-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-kirby-18">\n         [18]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guarnieri_7-1_13-2">\n        <a href="#cite_note-guarnieri_7-1-13">\n         [13]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <a href="/wiki/Alessandro_Volta" title="Alessandro Volta">\n        Alessandro Volta\n       </a>\n       \'s battery, or\n       <a href="/wiki/Voltaic_pile" title="Voltaic pile">\n        voltaic pile\n       </a>\n       , of 1800, made from alternating layers of zinc and copper, provided scientists with a more reliable source of electrical energy than the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrostatic_machine" title="Electrostatic machine">\n        electrostatic machines\n       </a>\n       previously used.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guarnieri_7-2_17-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-guarnieri_7-2-17">\n         [17]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kirby_18-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-kirby-18">\n         [18]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       The recognition of\n       <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetism" title="Electromagnetism">\n        electromagnetism\n       </a>\n       , the unity of electric and magnetic phenomena, is due to\n       <a href="/wiki/Hans_Christian_%C3%98rsted" title="Hans Christian \xc3\x98rsted">\n        Hans Christian \xc3\x98rsted\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/Andr%C3%A9-Marie_Amp%C3%A8re" title="Andr\xc3\xa9-Marie Amp\xc3\xa8re">\n        Andr\xc3\xa9-Marie Amp\xc3\xa8re\n       </a>\n       in 1819\xe2\x80\x931820.\n       <a href="/wiki/Michael_Faraday" title="Michael Faraday">\n        Michael Faraday\n       </a>\n       invented the\n       <a href="/wiki/Electric_motor" title="Electric motor">\n        electric motor\n       </a>\n       in 1821, and\n       <a href="/wiki/Georg_Ohm" title="Georg Ohm">\n        Georg Ohm\n       </a>\n       mathematically analysed the electrical circuit in 1827.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kirby_18-2">\n        <a href="#cite_note-kirby-18">\n         [18]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Electricity and magnetism (and light) were definitively linked by\n       <a href="/wiki/James_Clerk_Maxwell" title="James Clerk Maxwell">\n        James Clerk Maxwell\n       </a>\n       , in particular in his "\n       <a href="/wiki/On_Physical_Lines_of_Force" title="On Physical Lines of Force">\n        On Physical Lines of Force\n       </a>\n       " in 1861 and 1862.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">\n        <a href="#cite_note-19">\n         [19]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       While the early 19th century had seen rapid progress in electrical science, the late 19th century would see the greatest progress in\n       <a href="/wiki/Electrical_engineering" title="Electrical engineering">\n        electrical engineering\n       </a>\n       . Through such people as\n       <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Graham_Bell" title="Alexander Graham Bell">\n        Alexander Graham Bell\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Ott%C3%B3_Bl%C3%A1thy" title="Ott\xc3\xb3 Bl\xc3\xa1thy">\n        Ott\xc3\xb3 Bl\xc3\xa1thy\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Edison" title="Thomas Edison">\n        Thomas Edison\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Galileo_Ferraris" title="Galileo Ferraris">\n        Galileo Ferraris\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Heaviside" title="Oliver Heaviside">\n        Oliver Heaviside\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/%C3%81nyos_Jedlik" title="\xc3\x81nyos Jedlik">\n        \xc3\x81nyos Jedlik\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/William_Thomson,_1st_Baron_Kelvin" title="William Thomson, 1st Baron Kelvin">\n        William Thomson, 1st Baron Kelvin\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Charles_Algernon_Parsons" title="Charles Algernon Parsons">\n        Charles Algernon Parsons\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Werner_von_Siemens" title="Werner von Siemens">\n        Werner von Siemens\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Swan" title="Joseph Swan">\n        Joseph Swan\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Reginald_Fessenden" title="Reginald Fessenden">\n        Reginald Fessenden\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Nikola_Tesla" title="Nikola Tesla">\n        Nikola Tesla\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/George_Westinghouse" title="George Westinghouse">\n        George Westinghouse\n       </a>\n       , electricity turned from a scientific curiosity into an essential tool for modern life, becoming a driving force of the\n       <a href="/wiki/Second_Industrial_Revolution" title="Second Industrial Revolution">\n        Second Industrial Revolution\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">\n        <a href="#cite_note-20">\n         [20]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       In 1887,\n       <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Hertz" title="Heinrich Hertz">\n        Heinrich Hertz\n       </a>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-uniphysics_21-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-uniphysics-21">\n         [21]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">\n        :843\xe2\x80\x93844\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hertz1887_22-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Hertz1887-22">\n         [22]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       discovered that\n       <a href="/wiki/Electrode" title="Electrode">\n        electrodes\n       </a>\n       illuminated with ultraviolet light create\n       <a href="/wiki/Electric_spark" title="Electric spark">\n        electric sparks\n       </a>\n       more easily. In 1905,\n       <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">\n        Albert Einstein\n       </a>\n       published a paper that explained experimental data from the\n       <a href="/wiki/Photoelectric_effect" title="Photoelectric effect">\n        photoelectric effect\n       </a>\n       as being the result of light energy being carried in discrete quantized packets, energising electrons. This discovery led to the\n       <a href="/wiki/Quantum" title="Quantum">\n        quantum\n       </a>\n       revolution. Einstein was awarded the\n       <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Physics" title="Nobel Prize in Physics">\n        Nobel Prize in Physics\n       </a>\n       in 1921 for "his discovery of the law of the photoelectric effect".\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">\n        <a href="#cite_note-23">\n         [23]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       The photoelectric effect is also employed in\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photocell" title="Photocell">\n        photocells\n       </a>\n       such as can be found in\n       <a href="/wiki/Solar_panel" title="Solar panel">\n        solar panels\n       </a>\n       and this is frequently used to make electricity commercially.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The first\n       <a href="/wiki/Solid-state_electronics" title="Solid-state electronics">\n        solid-state device\n       </a>\n       was the "\n       <a href="/wiki/Cat%27s-whisker_detector" title="Cat\'s-whisker detector">\n        cat\'s-whisker detector\n       </a>\n       " first used in the 1900s in radio receivers. A whisker-like wire is placed lightly in contact with a solid crystal (such as a\n       <a href="/wiki/Germanium" title="Germanium">\n        germanium\n       </a>\n       crystal) in order to detect a\n       <a href="/wiki/Radio" title="Radio">\n        radio\n       </a>\n       signal by the contact junction effect.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">\n        <a href="#cite_note-24">\n         [24]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       In a solid-state component, the\n       <a href="/wiki/Electric_current" title="Electric current">\n        current\n       </a>\n       is confined to solid elements and compounds engineered specifically to switch and amplify it. Current flow can be understood in two forms: as negatively charged\n       <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">\n        electrons\n       </a>\n       , and as positively charged electron deficiencies called\n       <a href="/wiki/Electron_hole" title="Electron hole">\n        holes\n       </a>\n       . These charges and holes are understood in terms of quantum physics. The building material is most often a crystalline\n       <a href="/wiki/Semiconductor" title="Semiconductor">\n        semiconductor\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">\n        <a href="#cite_note-25">\n         [25]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">\n        <a href="#cite_note-26">\n         [26]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The solid-state device came into its own with the invention of the\n       <a href="/wiki/Transistor" title="Transistor">\n        transistor\n       </a>\n       in 1947. Common solid-state devices include\n       <a href="/wiki/Transistor" title="Transistor">\n        transistors\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Microprocessor" title="Microprocessor">\n        microprocessor\n       </a>\n       chips, and\n       <a href="/wiki/Random-access_memory" title="Random-access memory">\n        RAM\n       </a>\n       . A specialized type of RAM called\n       <a href="/wiki/Flash_memory" title="Flash memory">\n        flash RAM\n       </a>\n       is used in\n       <a href="/wiki/USB_flash_drive" title="USB flash drive">\n        USB flash drives\n       </a>\n       and more recently,\n       <a href="/wiki/Solid-state_drive" title="Solid-state drive">\n        solid-state drives\n       </a>\n       to replace mechanically rotating magnetic disc\n       <a href="/wiki/Hard_disk_drive" title="Hard disk drive">\n        hard disk drives\n       </a>\n       . Solid state devices became prevalent in the 1950s and the 1960s, during the transition from\n       <a href="/wiki/Vacuum_tube" title="Vacuum tube">\n        vacuum tubes\n       </a>\n       to semiconductor\n       <a href="/wiki/Diode" title="Diode">\n        diodes\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Transistor" title="Transistor">\n        transistors\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Integrated_circuit" title="Integrated circuit">\n        integrated circuit\n       </a>\n       (IC) and the\n       <a href="/wiki/Light-emitting_diode" title="Light-emitting diode">\n        light-emitting diode\n       </a>\n       (LED).\n      </p>\n      <h2>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Concepts">\n        Concepts\n       </span>\n      </h2>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Electric_charge">\n        Electric charge\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       Main article:\n       <a href="/wiki/Electric_charge" title="Electric charge">\n        Electric charge\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       See also:\n       <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">\n        electron\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">\n        proton\n       </a>\n       , and\n       <a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">\n        ion\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Electroscope.svg">\n         <img alt="A clear glass dome has an external electrode which connects through the glass to a pair of gold leaves. A charged rod touches the external electrode and makes the leaves repel." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1077" data-file-width="811" height="226" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Electroscope.svg/170px-Electroscope.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Electroscope.svg/255px-Electroscope.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Electroscope.svg/340px-Electroscope.svg.png 2x" width="170"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Electroscope.svg" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         Charge on a\n         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gold-leaf_electroscope" title="Gold-leaf electroscope">\n          gold-leaf electroscope\n         </a>\n         causes the leaves to visibly repel each other\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       The presence of charge gives rise to an electrostatic force: charges exert a\n       <a href="/wiki/Force" title="Force">\n        force\n       </a>\n       on each other, an effect that was known, though not understood, in antiquity.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-uniphysics_21-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-uniphysics-21">\n         [21]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">\n        :457\n       </sup>\n       A lightweight ball suspended from a string can be charged by touching it with a glass rod that has itself been charged by rubbing with a cloth. If a similar ball is charged by the same glass rod, it is found to repel the first: the charge acts to force the two balls apart. Two balls that are charged with a rubbed amber rod also repel each other. However, if one ball is charged by the glass rod, and the other by an amber rod, the two balls are found to attract each other. These phenomena were investigated in the late eighteenth century by\n       <a href="/wiki/Charles-Augustin_de_Coulomb" title="Charles-Augustin de Coulomb">\n        Charles-Augustin de Coulomb\n       </a>\n       , who deduced that charge manifests itself in two opposing forms. This discovery led to the well-known axiom:\n       <i>\n        like-charged objects repel and opposite-charged objects attract\n       </i>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-uniphysics_21-2">\n        <a href="#cite_note-uniphysics-21">\n         [21]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The force acts on the charged particles themselves, hence charge has a tendency to spread itself as evenly as possible over a conducting surface. The magnitude of the electromagnetic force, whether attractive or repulsive, is given by\n       <a href="/wiki/Coulomb%27s_law" title="Coulomb\'s law">\n        Coulomb\'s law\n       </a>\n       , which relates the force to the product of the charges and has an\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inverse-square" title="Inverse-square">\n        inverse-square\n       </a>\n       relation to the distance between them.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">\n        <a href="#cite_note-27">\n         [27]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Duffin_28-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Duffin-28">\n         [28]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">\n        :35\n       </sup>\n       The electromagnetic force is very strong, second only in strength to the\n       <a href="/wiki/Strong_interaction" title="Strong interaction">\n        strong interaction\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">\n        <a href="#cite_note-29">\n         [29]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       but unlike that force it operates over all distances.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Umashankar_30-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Umashankar-30">\n         [30]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       In comparison with the much weaker\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitational_force" title="Gravitational force">\n        gravitational force\n       </a>\n       , the electromagnetic force pushing two electrons apart is 10\n       <sup>\n        42\n       </sup>\n       times that of the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitation" title="Gravitation">\n        gravitational\n       </a>\n       attraction pulling them together.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hawking_31-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-hawking-31">\n         [31]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Study has shown that the origin of charge is from certain types of\n       <a href="/wiki/Subatomic_particle" title="Subatomic particle">\n        subatomic particles\n       </a>\n       which have the property of electric charge. Electric charge gives rise to and interacts with the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_force" title="Electromagnetic force">\n        electromagnetic force\n       </a>\n       , one of the four\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fundamental_force" title="Fundamental force">\n        fundamental forces\n       </a>\n       of nature. The most familiar carriers of electrical charge are the\n       <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">\n        electron\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">\n        proton\n       </a>\n       . Experiment has shown charge to be a\n       <a href="/wiki/Conserved_quantity" title="Conserved quantity">\n        conserved quantity\n       </a>\n       , that is, the net charge within an\n       <a href="/wiki/Isolated_system" title="Isolated system">\n        isolated system\n       </a>\n       will always remain constant regardless of any changes taking place within that system.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">\n        <a href="#cite_note-32">\n         [32]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Within the system, charge may be transferred between bodies, either by direct contact, or by passing along a conducting material, such as a wire.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Duffin_28-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Duffin-28">\n         [28]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">\n        :2\xe2\x80\x935\n       </sup>\n       The informal term\n       <a href="/wiki/Static_electricity" title="Static electricity">\n        static electricity\n       </a>\n       refers to the net presence (or \'imbalance\') of charge on a body, usually caused when dissimilar materials are rubbed together, transferring charge from one to the other.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The charge on electrons and protons is opposite in sign, hence an amount of charge may be expressed as being either negative or positive. By convention, the charge carried by electrons is deemed negative, and that by protons positive, a custom that originated with the work of\n       <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Franklin" title="Benjamin Franklin">\n        Benjamin Franklin\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">\n        <a href="#cite_note-33">\n         [33]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       The amount of charge is usually given the symbol\n       <i>\n        Q\n       </i>\n       and expressed in\n       <a href="/wiki/Coulomb" title="Coulomb">\n        coulombs\n       </a>\n       ;\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">\n        <a href="#cite_note-34">\n         [34]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       each electron carries the same charge of approximately \xe2\x88\x921.6022\xc3\x9710\n       <sup>\n        \xe2\x88\x9219\n       </sup>\n       <a href="/wiki/Coulomb" title="Coulomb">\n        coulomb\n       </a>\n       . The proton has a charge that is equal and opposite, and thus +1.6022\xc3\x9710\n       <sup>\n        \xe2\x88\x9219\n       </sup>\n       coulomb. Charge is possessed not just by\n       <a href="/wiki/Matter" title="Matter">\n        matter\n       </a>\n       , but also by\n       <a href="/wiki/Antimatter" title="Antimatter">\n        antimatter\n       </a>\n       , each\n       <a href="/wiki/Antiparticle" title="Antiparticle">\n        antiparticle\n       </a>\n       bearing an equal and opposite charge to its corresponding particle.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">\n        <a href="#cite_note-35">\n         [35]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Charge can be measured by a number of means, an early instrument being the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gold-leaf_electroscope" title="Gold-leaf electroscope">\n        gold-leaf electroscope\n       </a>\n       , which although still in use for classroom demonstrations, has been superseded by the electronic\n       <a href="/wiki/Electrometer" title="Electrometer">\n        electrometer\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Duffin_28-2">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Duffin-28">\n         [28]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">\n        :2\xe2\x80\x935\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Electric_current">\n        Electric current\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       Main article:\n       <a href="/wiki/Electric_current" title="Electric current">\n        Electric current\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       The movement of electric charge is known as an\n       <a href="/wiki/Electric_current" title="Electric current">\n        electric current\n       </a>\n       , the intensity of which is usually measured in\n       <a href="/wiki/Ampere" title="Ampere">\n        amperes\n       </a>\n       . Current can consist of any moving charged particles; most commonly these are electrons, but any charge in motion constitutes a current. Electric current can flow through some things,\n       <a href="/wiki/Electrical_conductor" title="Electrical conductor">\n        electrical conductors\n       </a>\n       , but will not flow through an\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_insulator" title="Electrical insulator">\n        electrical insulator\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">\n        <a href="#cite_note-36">\n         [36]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       By historical convention, a positive current is defined as having the same direction of flow as any positive charge it contains, or to flow from the most positive part of a circuit to the most negative part. Current defined in this manner is called\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conventional_current" title="Conventional current">\n        conventional current\n       </a>\n       . The motion of negatively charged electrons around an\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electric_circuit" title="Electric circuit">\n        electric circuit\n       </a>\n       , one of the most familiar forms of current, is thus deemed positive in the\n       <i>\n        opposite\n       </i>\n       direction to that of the electrons.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">\n        <a href="#cite_note-37">\n         [37]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       However, depending on the conditions, an electric current can consist of a flow of\n       <a href="/wiki/Charged_particle" title="Charged particle">\n        charged particles\n       </a>\n       in either direction, or even in both directions at once. The positive-to-negative convention is widely used to simplify this situation.\n      </p>\n      <div class="thumb tleft">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lichtbogen_3000_Volt.jpg">\n         <img alt="Two metal wires form an inverted V shape. A blindingly bright orange-white electric arc flows between their tips." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="520" data-file-width="600" height="191" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Lichtbogen_3000_Volt.jpg/220px-Lichtbogen_3000_Volt.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Lichtbogen_3000_Volt.jpg/330px-Lichtbogen_3000_Volt.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Lichtbogen_3000_Volt.jpg/440px-Lichtbogen_3000_Volt.jpg 2x" width="220"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lichtbogen_3000_Volt.jpg" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         An\n         <a href="/wiki/Electric_arc" title="Electric arc">\n          electric arc\n         </a>\n         provides an energetic demonstration of electric current\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       The process by which electric current passes through a material is termed\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_conduction" title="Electrical conduction">\n        electrical conduction\n       </a>\n       , and its nature varies with that of the charged particles and the material through which they are travelling. Examples of electric currents include metallic conduction, where electrons flow through a\n       <a href="/wiki/Electrical_conductor" title="Electrical conductor">\n        conductor\n       </a>\n       such as metal, and\n       <a href="/wiki/Electrolysis" title="Electrolysis">\n        electrolysis\n       </a>\n       , where\n       <a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">\n        ions\n       </a>\n       (charged\n       <a href="/wiki/Atom" title="Atom">\n        atoms\n       </a>\n       ) flow through liquids, or through\n       <a href="/wiki/Plasma_(physics)" title="Plasma (physics)">\n        plasmas\n       </a>\n       such as electrical sparks. While the particles themselves can move quite slowly, sometimes with an average\n       <a href="/wiki/Drift_velocity" title="Drift velocity">\n        drift velocity\n       </a>\n       only fractions of a millimetre per second,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Duffin_28-3">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Duffin-28">\n         [28]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">\n        :17\n       </sup>\n       the\n       <a href="/wiki/Electric_field" title="Electric field">\n        electric field\n       </a>\n       that drives them itself propagates at close to the\n       <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_light" title="Speed of light">\n        speed of light\n       </a>\n       , enabling electrical signals to pass rapidly along wires.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">\n        <a href="#cite_note-38">\n         [38]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Current causes several observable effects, which historically were the means of recognising its presence. That water could be decomposed by the current from a voltaic pile was discovered by\n       <a href="/wiki/William_Nicholson_(chemist)" title="William Nicholson (chemist)">\n        Nicholson\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Carlisle" title="Anthony Carlisle">\n        Carlisle\n       </a>\n       in 1800, a process now known as\n       <a href="/wiki/Electrolysis" title="Electrolysis">\n        electrolysis\n       </a>\n       . Their work was greatly expanded upon by\n       <a href="/wiki/Michael_Faraday" title="Michael Faraday">\n        Michael Faraday\n       </a>\n       in 1833. Current through a\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_resistance" title="Electrical resistance">\n        resistance\n       </a>\n       causes localised heating, an effect\n       <a href="/wiki/James_Prescott_Joule" title="James Prescott Joule">\n        James Prescott Joule\n       </a>\n       studied mathematically in 1840.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Duffin_28-4">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Duffin-28">\n         [28]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">\n        :23\xe2\x80\x9324\n       </sup>\n       One of the most important discoveries relating to current was made accidentally by\n       <a href="/wiki/Hans_Christian_%C3%98rsted" title="Hans Christian \xc3\x98rsted">\n        Hans Christian \xc3\x98rsted\n       </a>\n       in 1820, when, while preparing a lecture, he witnessed the current in a wire disturbing the needle of a magnetic compass.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-berkson_39-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-berkson-39">\n         [39]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       He had discovered\n       <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetism" title="Electromagnetism">\n        electromagnetism\n       </a>\n       , a fundamental interaction between electricity and magnetics. The level of electromagnetic emissions generated by\n       <a href="/wiki/Electric_arc" title="Electric arc">\n        electric arcing\n       </a>\n       is high enough to produce\n       <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_interference" title="Electromagnetic interference">\n        electromagnetic interference\n       </a>\n       , which can be detrimental to the workings of adjacent equipment.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">\n        <a href="#cite_note-40">\n         [40]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       In engineering or household applications, current is often described as being either\n       <a href="/wiki/Direct_current" title="Direct current">\n        direct current\n       </a>\n       (DC) or\n       <a href="/wiki/Alternating_current" title="Alternating current">\n        alternating current\n       </a>\n       (AC). These terms refer to how the current varies in time. Direct current, as produced by example from a\n       <a href="/wiki/Battery_(electricity)" title="Battery (electricity)">\n        battery\n       </a>\n       and required by most\n       <a href="/wiki/Electronics" title="Electronics">\n        electronic\n       </a>\n       devices, is a unidirectional flow from the positive part of a circuit to the negative.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bird_41-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-bird-41">\n         [41]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">\n        :11\n       </sup>\n       If, as is most common, this flow is carried by electrons, they will be travelling in the opposite direction. Alternating current is any current that reverses direction repeatedly; almost always this takes the form of a\n       <a href="/wiki/Sine_wave" title="Sine wave">\n        sine wave\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bird_41-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-bird-41">\n         [41]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">\n        :206\xe2\x80\x93207\n       </sup>\n       Alternating current thus pulses back and forth within a conductor without the charge moving any net distance over time. The time-averaged value of an alternating current is zero, but it delivers energy in first one direction, and then the reverse. Alternating current is affected by electrical properties that are not observed under\n       <a href="/wiki/Steady_state" title="Steady state">\n        steady state\n       </a>\n       direct current, such as\n       <a href="/wiki/Inductance" title="Inductance">\n        inductance\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/Capacitance" title="Capacitance">\n        capacitance\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bird_41-2">\n        <a href="#cite_note-bird-41">\n         [41]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">\n        :223\xe2\x80\x93225\n       </sup>\n       These properties however can become important when circuitry is subjected to\n       <a href="/wiki/Transient_response" title="Transient response">\n        transients\n       </a>\n       , such as when first energised.\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Electric_field">\n        Electric field\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       Main article:\n       <a href="/wiki/Electric_field" title="Electric field">\n        Electric field\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       See also:\n       <a href="/wiki/Electrostatics" title="Electrostatics">\n        Electrostatics\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       The concept of the electric\n       <a href="/wiki/Field_(physics)" title="Field (physics)">\n        field\n       </a>\n       was introduced by\n       <a href="/wiki/Michael_Faraday" title="Michael Faraday">\n        Michael Faraday\n       </a>\n       . An electric field is created by a charged body in the space that surrounds it, and results in a force exerted on any other charges placed within the field. The electric field acts between two charges in a similar manner to the way that the gravitational field acts between two\n       <a href="/wiki/Mass" title="Mass">\n        masses\n       </a>\n       , and like it, extends towards infinity and shows an inverse square relationship with distance.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Umashankar_30-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Umashankar-30">\n         [30]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       However, there is an important difference. Gravity always acts in attraction, drawing two masses together, while the electric field can result in either attraction or repulsion. Since large bodies such as planets generally carry no net charge, the electric field at a distance is usually zero. Thus gravity is the dominant force at distance in the universe, despite being much weaker.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hawking_31-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-hawking-31">\n         [31]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:VFPt_image_charge_plane_horizontal.svg">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="600" height="183" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/VFPt_image_charge_plane_horizontal.svg/220px-VFPt_image_charge_plane_horizontal.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/VFPt_image_charge_plane_horizontal.svg/330px-VFPt_image_charge_plane_horizontal.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/VFPt_image_charge_plane_horizontal.svg/440px-VFPt_image_charge_plane_horizontal.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:VFPt_image_charge_plane_horizontal.svg" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         Field lines emanating from a positive charge above a plane conductor\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       An electric field generally varies in space,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">\n        <a href="#cite_note-42">\n         [42]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       and its strength at any one point is defined as the force (per unit charge) that would be felt by a stationary, negligible charge if placed at that point.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-uniphysics_21-3">\n        <a href="#cite_note-uniphysics-21">\n         [21]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">\n        :469\xe2\x80\x93470\n       </sup>\n       The conceptual charge, termed a \'\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Test_charge" title="Test charge">\n        test charge\n       </a>\n       \', must be vanishingly small to prevent its own electric field disturbing the main field and must also be stationary to prevent the effect of\n       <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">\n        magnetic fields\n       </a>\n       . As the electric field is defined in terms of\n       <a href="/wiki/Force" title="Force">\n        force\n       </a>\n       , and force is a\n       <a href="/wiki/Euclidean_vector" title="Euclidean vector">\n        vector\n       </a>\n       , so it follows that an electric field is also a vector, having both\n       <a href="/wiki/Magnitude_(mathematics)" title="Magnitude (mathematics)">\n        magnitude\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Direction_(geometry)" title="Direction (geometry)">\n        direction\n       </a>\n       . Specifically, it is a\n       <a href="/wiki/Vector_field" title="Vector field">\n        vector field\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-uniphysics_21-4">\n        <a href="#cite_note-uniphysics-21">\n         [21]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">\n        :469\xe2\x80\x93470\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The study of electric fields created by stationary charges is called\n       <a href="/wiki/Electrostatics" title="Electrostatics">\n        electrostatics\n       </a>\n       . The field may be visualised by a set of imaginary lines whose direction at any point is the same as that of the field. This concept was introduced by Faraday,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-elec_princ_p73_43-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-elec_princ_p73-43">\n         [43]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       whose term \'\n       <a href="/wiki/Line_of_force" title="Line of force">\n        lines of force\n       </a>\n       \' still sometimes sees use. The field lines are the paths that a point positive charge would seek to make as it was forced to move within the field; they are however an imaginary concept with no physical existence, and the field permeates all the intervening space between the lines.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-elec_princ_p73_43-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-elec_princ_p73-43">\n         [43]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Field lines emanating from stationary charges have several key properties: first, that they originate at positive charges and terminate at negative charges; second, that they must enter any good conductor at right angles, and third, that they may never cross nor close in on themselves.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-uniphysics_21-5">\n        <a href="#cite_note-uniphysics-21">\n         [21]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">\n        :479\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       A hollow conducting body carries all its charge on its outer surface. The field is therefore zero at all places inside the body.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Duffin_28-5">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Duffin-28">\n         [28]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">\n        :88\n       </sup>\n       This is the operating principal of the\n       <a href="/wiki/Faraday_cage" title="Faraday cage">\n        Faraday cage\n       </a>\n       , a conducting metal shell which isolates its interior from outside electrical effects.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The principles of electrostatics are important when designing items of\n       <a href="/wiki/High_voltage" title="High voltage">\n        high-voltage\n       </a>\n       equipment. There is a finite limit to the electric field strength that may be withstood by any medium. Beyond this point,\n       <a href="/wiki/Electrical_breakdown" title="Electrical breakdown">\n        electrical breakdown\n       </a>\n       occurs and an\n       <a href="/wiki/Electric_arc" title="Electric arc">\n        electric arc\n       </a>\n       causes flashover between the charged parts. Air, for example, tends to arc across small gaps at electric field strengths which exceed 30\xc2\xa0kV per centimetre. Over larger gaps, its breakdown strength is weaker, perhaps 1\xc2\xa0kV per centimetre.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hv_eng_44-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-hv_eng-44">\n         [44]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       The most visible natural occurrence of this is\n       <a href="/wiki/Lightning" title="Lightning">\n        lightning\n       </a>\n       , caused when charge becomes separated in the clouds by rising columns of air, and raises the electric field in the air to greater than it can withstand. The voltage of a large lightning cloud may be as high as 100\xc2\xa0MV and have discharge energies as great as 250\xc2\xa0kWh.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">\n        <a href="#cite_note-45">\n         [45]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The field strength is greatly affected by nearby conducting objects, and it is particularly intense when it is forced to curve around sharply pointed objects. This principle is exploited in the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lightning_conductor" title="Lightning conductor">\n        lightning conductor\n       </a>\n       , the sharp spike of which acts to encourage the lightning stroke to develop there, rather than to the building it serves to protect\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nahin2002_46-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Nahin2002-46">\n         [46]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">\n        :155\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Electric_potential">\n        Electric potential\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       Main article:\n       <a href="/wiki/Electric_potential" title="Electric potential">\n        Electric potential\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       See also:\n       <a href="/wiki/Voltage" title="Voltage">\n        Voltage\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/Battery_(electricity)" title="Battery (electricity)">\n        Battery (electricity)\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Panasonic-oxyride.jpg">\n         <img alt="Two AA batteries each have a plus sign marked at one end." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="618" height="273" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Panasonic-oxyride.jpg/220px-Panasonic-oxyride.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Panasonic-oxyride.jpg/330px-Panasonic-oxyride.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Panasonic-oxyride.jpg/440px-Panasonic-oxyride.jpg 2x" width="220"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Panasonic-oxyride.jpg" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         A pair of\n         <a href="/wiki/AA_battery" title="AA battery">\n          AA cells\n         </a>\n         . The +\xc2\xa0sign indicates the polarity of the potential difference between the battery terminals.\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       The concept of electric potential is closely linked to that of the electric field. A small charge placed within an electric field experiences a force, and to have brought that charge to that point against the force requires\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mechanical_work" title="Mechanical work">\n        work\n       </a>\n       . The electric potential at any point is defined as the energy required to bring a unit test charge from an\n       <a href="/wiki/Infinity" title="Infinity">\n        infinite distance\n       </a>\n       slowly to that point. It is usually measured in\n       <a href="/wiki/Volt" title="Volt">\n        volts\n       </a>\n       , and one volt is the potential for which one\n       <a href="/wiki/Joule" title="Joule">\n        joule\n       </a>\n       of work must be expended to bring a charge of one\n       <a href="/wiki/Coulomb" title="Coulomb">\n        coulomb\n       </a>\n       from infinity.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-uniphysics_21-6">\n        <a href="#cite_note-uniphysics-21">\n         [21]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">\n        :494\xe2\x80\x93498\n       </sup>\n       This definition of potential, while formal, has little practical application, and a more useful concept is that of\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electric_potential_difference" title="Electric potential difference">\n        electric potential difference\n       </a>\n       , and is the energy required to move a unit charge between two specified points. An electric field has the special property that it is\n       <i>\n        <a href="/wiki/Conservative_force" title="Conservative force">\n         conservative\n        </a>\n       </i>\n       , which means that the path taken by the test charge is irrelevant: all paths between two specified points expend the same energy, and thus a unique value for potential difference may be stated.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-uniphysics_21-7">\n        <a href="#cite_note-uniphysics-21">\n         [21]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">\n        :494\xe2\x80\x93498\n       </sup>\n       The volt is so strongly identified as the unit of choice for measurement and description of electric potential difference that the term\n       <a href="/wiki/Voltage" title="Voltage">\n        voltage\n       </a>\n       sees greater everyday usage.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       For practical purposes, it is useful to define a common reference point to which potentials may be expressed and compared. While this could be at infinity, a much more useful reference is the\n       <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">\n        Earth\n       </a>\n       itself, which is assumed to be at the same potential everywhere. This reference point naturally takes the name\n       <a href="/wiki/Ground_(electricity)" title="Ground (electricity)">\n        earth\n       </a>\n       or\n       <a href="/wiki/Ground_(electricity)" title="Ground (electricity)">\n        ground\n       </a>\n       . Earth is assumed to be an infinite source of equal amounts of positive and negative charge, and is therefore electrically uncharged\xe2\x80\x94and unchargeable.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">\n        <a href="#cite_note-47">\n         [47]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Electric potential is a\n       <a href="/wiki/Scalar_(physics)" title="Scalar (physics)">\n        scalar quantity\n       </a>\n       , that is, it has only magnitude and not direction. It may be viewed as analogous to\n       <a href="/wiki/Height" title="Height">\n        height\n       </a>\n       : just as a released object will fall through a difference in heights caused by a gravitational field, so a charge will \'fall\' across the voltage caused by an electric field.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">\n        <a href="#cite_note-48">\n         [48]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       As relief maps show\n       <a href="/wiki/Contour_line" title="Contour line">\n        contour lines\n       </a>\n       marking points of equal height, a set of lines marking points of equal potential (known as\n       <a href="/wiki/Equipotential" title="Equipotential">\n        equipotentials\n       </a>\n       ) may be drawn around an electrostatically charged object. The equipotentials cross all lines of force at right angles. They must also lie parallel to a\n       <a href="/wiki/Electrical_conductor" title="Electrical conductor">\n        conductor\n       </a>\n       \'s surface, otherwise this would produce a force that will move the charge carriers to even the potential of the surface.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The electric field was formally defined as the force exerted per unit charge, but the concept of potential allows for a more useful and equivalent definition: the electric field is the local\n       <a href="/wiki/Gradient" title="Gradient">\n        gradient\n       </a>\n       of the electric potential. Usually expressed in volts\xc2\xa0per\xc2\xa0metre, the vector direction of the field is the line of greatest slope of potential, and where the equipotentials lie closest together.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Duffin_28-6">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Duffin-28">\n         [28]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">\n        :60\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Electromagnets">\n        Electromagnets\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       Main article:\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electromagnets" title="Electromagnets">\n        Electromagnets\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <div class="thumb tleft">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Electromagnetism.svg">\n         <img alt="A wire carries a current towards the reader. Concentric circles representing the magnetic field circle anticlockwise around the wire, as viewed by the reader." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="709" data-file-width="651" height="240" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Electromagnetism.svg/220px-Electromagnetism.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Electromagnetism.svg/330px-Electromagnetism.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Electromagnetism.svg/440px-Electromagnetism.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Electromagnetism.svg" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         Magnetic field circles around a current\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       \xc3\x98rsted\'s discovery in 1821 that a\n       <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">\n        magnetic field\n       </a>\n       existed around all sides of a wire carrying an electric current indicated that there was a direct relationship between electricity and magnetism. Moreover, the interaction seemed different from gravitational and electrostatic forces, the two forces of nature then known. The force on the compass needle did not direct it to or away from the current-carrying wire, but acted at right angles to it.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-berkson_39-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-berkson-39">\n         [39]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       \xc3\x98rsted\'s slightly obscure words were that "the electric conflict acts in a revolving manner." The force also depended on the direction of the current, for if the flow was reversed, then the force did too.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">\n        <a href="#cite_note-49">\n         [49]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       \xc3\x98rsted did not fully understand his discovery, but he observed the effect was reciprocal: a current exerts a force on a magnet, and a magnetic field exerts a force on a current. The phenomenon was further investigated by\n       <a href="/wiki/Andr%C3%A9-Marie_Amp%C3%A8re" title="Andr\xc3\xa9-Marie Amp\xc3\xa8re">\n        Amp\xc3\xa8re\n       </a>\n       , who discovered that two parallel current-carrying wires exerted a force upon each other: two wires conducting currents in the same direction are attracted to each other, while wires containing currents in opposite directions are forced apart.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-elec_princ_92-93_50-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-elec_princ_92-93-50">\n         [50]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       The interaction is mediated by the magnetic field each current produces and forms the basis for the international\n       <a href="/wiki/Ampere#Definition" title="Ampere">\n        definition of the
