ampere\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-elec_princ_92-93_50-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-elec_princ_92-93-50">\n         [50]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Electric_motor_cycle_3.png">\n         <img alt="A cut-away diagram of a small electric motor" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="600" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Electric_motor_cycle_3.png/220px-Electric_motor_cycle_3.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Electric_motor_cycle_3.png/330px-Electric_motor_cycle_3.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Electric_motor_cycle_3.png/440px-Electric_motor_cycle_3.png 2x" width="220"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Electric_motor_cycle_3.png" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         The electric motor exploits an important effect of electromagnetism: a current through a magnetic field experiences a force at right angles to both the field and current\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       This relationship between magnetic fields and currents is extremely important, for it led to Michael Faraday\'s invention of the\n       <a href="/wiki/Electric_motor" title="Electric motor">\n        electric motor\n       </a>\n       in 1821. Faraday\'s\n       <a href="/wiki/Homopolar_motor" title="Homopolar motor">\n        homopolar motor\n       </a>\n       consisted of a\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Permanent_magnet" title="Permanent magnet">\n        permanent magnet\n       </a>\n       sitting in a pool of\n       <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(element)" title="Mercury (element)">\n        mercury\n       </a>\n       . A current was allowed through a wire suspended from a pivot above the magnet and dipped into the mercury. The magnet exerted a tangential force on the wire, making it circle around the magnet for as long as the current was maintained.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iet_faraday_51-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-iet_faraday-51">\n         [51]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Experimentation by Faraday in 1831 revealed that a wire moving perpendicular to a magnetic field developed a potential difference between its ends. Further analysis of this process, known as\n       <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_induction" title="Electromagnetic induction">\n        electromagnetic induction\n       </a>\n       , enabled him to state the principle, now known as\n       <a href="/wiki/Faraday%27s_law_of_induction" title="Faraday\'s law of induction">\n        Faraday\'s law of induction\n       </a>\n       , that the potential difference induced in a closed circuit is proportional to the rate of change of\n       <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_flux" title="Magnetic flux">\n        magnetic flux\n       </a>\n       through the loop. Exploitation of this discovery enabled him to invent the first\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_generator" title="Electrical generator">\n        electrical generator\n       </a>\n       in 1831, in which he converted the mechanical energy of a rotating copper disc to electrical energy.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iet_faraday_51-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-iet_faraday-51">\n         [51]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Faraday%27s_disc" title="Faraday\'s disc">\n        Faraday\'s disc\n       </a>\n       was inefficient and of no use as a practical generator, but it showed the possibility of generating electric power using magnetism, a possibility that would be taken up by those that followed on from his work.\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Electrochemistry">\n        Electrochemistry\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Volta-and-napoleon.PNG">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="256" data-file-width="258" height="218" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Volta-and-napoleon.PNG/220px-Volta-and-napoleon.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/28/Volta-and-napoleon.PNG 1.5x" width="220"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Volta-and-napoleon.PNG" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">\n          Italian\n         </a>\n         <a href="/wiki/Physicist" title="Physicist">\n          physicist\n         </a>\n         <a href="/wiki/Alessandro_Volta" title="Alessandro Volta">\n          Alessandro Volta\n         </a>\n         showing his\n         <i>\n          "\n          <a href="/wiki/Battery_(electricity)" title="Battery (electricity)">\n           battery\n          </a>\n          "\n         </i>\n         to\n         <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">\n          French\n         </a>\n         <a href="/wiki/Emperor" title="Emperor">\n          emperor\n         </a>\n         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Napoleon_I_of_France" title="Napoleon I of France">\n          Napoleon Bonaparte\n         </a>\n         in the early 19th century.\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       Main article:\n       <a href="/wiki/Electrochemistry" title="Electrochemistry">\n        Electrochemistry\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       The ability of chemical reactions to produce electricity, and conversely the ability of electricity to drive chemical reactions has a wide array of uses.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Electrochemistry has always been an important part of electricity. From the initial invention of the Voltaic pile,\n       <a href="/wiki/Electrochemical_cell" title="Electrochemical cell">\n        electrochemical cells\n       </a>\n       have evolved into the many different types of batteries, electroplating and electrolysis cells.\n       <a href="/wiki/Aluminium" title="Aluminium">\n        Aluminium\n       </a>\n       is produced in vast quantities this way, and many portable devices are electrically powered using rechargeable cells.\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Electric_circuits">\n        Electric circuits\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       Main article:\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electric_circuit" title="Electric circuit">\n        Electric circuit\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ohms_law_voltage_source.svg">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="209" data-file-width="285" height="161" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Ohms_law_voltage_source.svg/220px-Ohms_law_voltage_source.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Ohms_law_voltage_source.svg/330px-Ohms_law_voltage_source.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Ohms_law_voltage_source.svg/440px-Ohms_law_voltage_source.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ohms_law_voltage_source.svg" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         A basic\n         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electric_circuit" title="Electric circuit">\n          electric circuit\n         </a>\n         . The\n         <a href="/wiki/Voltage_source" title="Voltage source">\n          voltage source\n         </a>\n         <i>\n          V\n         </i>\n         on the left drives a\n         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Current_(electricity)" title="Current (electricity)">\n          current\n         </a>\n         <i>\n          I\n         </i>\n         around the circuit, delivering\n         <a href="/wiki/Electrical_energy" title="Electrical energy">\n          electrical energy\n         </a>\n         into the\n         <a href="/wiki/Resistor" title="Resistor">\n          resistor\n         </a>\n         <i>\n          R\n         </i>\n         . From the resistor, the current returns to the source, completing the circuit.\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       An electric circuit is an interconnection of electric components such that electric charge is made to flow along a closed path (a circuit), usually to perform some useful task.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The components in an electric circuit can take many forms, which can include elements such as\n       <a href="/wiki/Resistor" title="Resistor">\n        resistors\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Capacitor" title="Capacitor">\n        capacitors\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Switch" title="Switch">\n        switches\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Transformer" title="Transformer">\n        transformers\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/Electronics" title="Electronics">\n        electronics\n       </a>\n       .\n       <a href="/wiki/Electronic_circuit" title="Electronic circuit">\n        Electronic circuits\n       </a>\n       contain\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Active_component" title="Active component">\n        active components\n       </a>\n       , usually\n       <a href="/wiki/Semiconductor" title="Semiconductor">\n        semiconductors\n       </a>\n       , and typically exhibit\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non-linear" title="Non-linear">\n        non-linear\n       </a>\n       behaviour, requiring complex analysis. The simplest electric components are those that are termed\n       <a href="/wiki/Passivity_(engineering)" title="Passivity (engineering)">\n        passive\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Linear" title="Linear">\n        linear\n       </a>\n       : while they may temporarily store energy, they contain no sources of it, and exhibit linear responses to stimuli.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alexander_52-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Alexander-52">\n         [52]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">\n        :15\xe2\x80\x9316\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The\n       <a href="/wiki/Resistor" title="Resistor">\n        resistor\n       </a>\n       is perhaps the simplest of passive circuit elements: as its name suggests, it\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_resistance" title="Electrical resistance">\n        resists\n       </a>\n       the current through it, dissipating its energy as heat. The resistance is a consequence of the motion of charge through a conductor: in metals, for example, resistance is primarily due to collisions between electrons and ions.\n       <a href="/wiki/Ohm%27s_law" title="Ohm\'s law">\n        Ohm\'s law\n       </a>\n       is a basic law of\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Circuit_theory" title="Circuit theory">\n        circuit theory\n       </a>\n       , stating that the current passing through a resistance is directly proportional to the potential difference across it. The resistance of most materials is relatively constant over a range of temperatures and currents; materials under these conditions are known as \'ohmic\'. The\n       <a href="/wiki/Ohm" title="Ohm">\n        ohm\n       </a>\n       , the unit of resistance, was named in honour of\n       <a href="/wiki/Georg_Ohm" title="Georg Ohm">\n        Georg Ohm\n       </a>\n       , and is symbolised by the Greek letter \xce\xa9. 1\xc2\xa0\xce\xa9 is the resistance that will produce a potential difference of one volt in response to a current of one amp.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alexander_52-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Alexander-52">\n         [52]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">\n        :30\xe2\x80\x9335\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The\n       <a href="/wiki/Capacitor" title="Capacitor">\n        capacitor\n       </a>\n       is a development of the Leyden jar and is a device that can store charge, and thereby storing electrical energy in the resulting field. It consists of two conducting plates separated by a thin\n       <a href="/wiki/Insulator_(electricity)" title="Insulator (electricity)">\n        insulating\n       </a>\n       <a href="/wiki/Dielectric" title="Dielectric">\n        dielectric\n       </a>\n       layer; in practice, thin metal foils are coiled together, increasing the surface area per unit volume and therefore the\n       <a href="/wiki/Capacitance" title="Capacitance">\n        capacitance\n       </a>\n       . The unit of capacitance is the\n       <a href="/wiki/Farad" title="Farad">\n        farad\n       </a>\n       , named after\n       <a href="/wiki/Michael_Faraday" title="Michael Faraday">\n        Michael Faraday\n       </a>\n       , and given the symbol\n       <i>\n        F\n       </i>\n       : one farad is the capacitance that develops a potential difference of one volt when it stores a charge of one coulomb. A capacitor connected to a voltage supply initially causes a current as it accumulates charge; this current will however decay in time as the capacitor fills, eventually falling to zero. A capacitor will therefore not permit a\n       <a href="/wiki/Steady_state" title="Steady state">\n        steady state\n       </a>\n       current, but instead blocks it.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alexander_52-2">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Alexander-52">\n         [52]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">\n        :216\xe2\x80\x93220\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The\n       <a href="/wiki/Inductor" title="Inductor">\n        inductor\n       </a>\n       is a conductor, usually a coil of wire, that stores energy in a magnetic field in response to the current through it. When the current changes, the magnetic field does too,\n       <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_induction" title="Electromagnetic induction">\n        inducing\n       </a>\n       a voltage between the ends of the conductor. The induced voltage is proportional to the\n       <a href="/wiki/Time_derivative" title="Time derivative">\n        time rate of change\n       </a>\n       of the current. The constant of proportionality is termed the\n       <a href="/wiki/Inductance" title="Inductance">\n        inductance\n       </a>\n       . The unit of inductance is the\n       <a href="/wiki/Henry_(unit)" title="Henry (unit)">\n        henry\n       </a>\n       , named after\n       <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Henry" title="Joseph Henry">\n        Joseph Henry\n       </a>\n       , a contemporary of Faraday. One henry is the inductance that will induce a potential difference of one volt if the current through it changes at a rate of one ampere per second. The inductor\'s behaviour is in some regards converse to that of the capacitor: it will freely allow an unchanging current, but opposes a rapidly changing one.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alexander_52-3">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Alexander-52">\n         [52]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">\n        :226\xe2\x80\x93229\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Electric_power">\n        Electric power\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       Main article:\n       <a href="/wiki/Electric_power" title="Electric power">\n        electric power\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       Electric power is the rate at which\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electric_energy" title="Electric energy">\n        electric energy\n       </a>\n       is transferred by an\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electric_circuit" title="Electric circuit">\n        electric circuit\n       </a>\n       . The\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SI" title="SI">\n        SI\n       </a>\n       unit of\n       <a href="/wiki/Power_(physics)" title="Power (physics)">\n        power\n       </a>\n       is the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Watt_(unit)" title="Watt (unit)">\n        watt\n       </a>\n       , one\n       <a href="/wiki/Joule" title="Joule">\n        joule\n       </a>\n       per\n       <a href="/wiki/Second" title="Second">\n        second\n       </a>\n       .\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Electric power, like\n       <a href="/wiki/Power_(physics)" title="Power (physics)">\n        mechanical power\n       </a>\n       , is the rate of doing\n       <a href="/wiki/Work_(electrical)" title="Work (electrical)">\n        work\n       </a>\n       , measured in\n       <a href="/wiki/Watt" title="Watt">\n        watts\n       </a>\n       , and represented by the letter\n       <i>\n        P\n       </i>\n       . The term\n       <i>\n        wattage\n       </i>\n       is used colloquially to mean "electric power in watts." The electric power in\n       <a href="/wiki/Watt" title="Watt">\n        watts\n       </a>\n       produced by an electric current\n       <i>\n        I\n       </i>\n       consisting of a charge of\n       <i>\n        Q\n       </i>\n       coulombs every\n       <i>\n        t\n       </i>\n       seconds passing through an\n       <a href="/wiki/Electric_potential" title="Electric potential">\n        electric potential\n       </a>\n       (\n       <a href="/wiki/Voltage" title="Voltage">\n        voltage\n       </a>\n       ) difference of\n       <i>\n        V\n       </i>\n       is\n      </p>\n      <dl>\n       <dd>\n        <span class="mwe-math-element">\n         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">\n          <math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n           <semantics>\n            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n              <mi>\n               P\n              </mi>\n              <mo>\n               =\n              </mo>\n              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n               <mtext>\n                work done per unit time\n               </mtext>\n              </mrow>\n              <mo>\n               =\n              </mo>\n              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n               <mfrac>\n                <mrow>\n                 <mi>\n                  Q\n                 </mi>\n                 <mi>\n                  V\n                 </mi>\n                </mrow>\n                <mi>\n                 t\n                </mi>\n               </mfrac>\n              </mrow>\n              <mo>\n               =\n              </mo>\n              <mi>\n               I\n              </mi>\n              <mi>\n               V\n              </mi>\n              <mspace width="thinmathspace">\n              </mspace>\n             </mstyle>\n            </mrow>\n            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\n             {\\displaystyle P={\\text{work done per unit time}}={\\frac {QV}{t}}=IV\\,}\n            </annotation>\n           </semantics>\n          </math>\n         </span>\n         <img alt="P = \\text{work done per unit time} = \\frac {QV}{t} = IV \\," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/53a84ac821c54d1255c69e9a140f728a54fb8158" style="vertical-align: -1.838ex; width:43.106ex; height:5.509ex;"/>\n        </span>\n       </dd>\n      </dl>\n      <p>\n       where\n      </p>\n      <dl>\n       <dd>\n        <i>\n         Q\n        </i>\n        is electric charge in\n        <a href="/wiki/Coulomb" title="Coulomb">\n         coulombs\n        </a>\n       </dd>\n       <dd>\n        <i>\n         t\n        </i>\n        is time in seconds\n       </dd>\n       <dd>\n        <i>\n         I\n        </i>\n        is electric current in\n        <a href="/wiki/Ampere" title="Ampere">\n         amperes\n        </a>\n       </dd>\n       <dd>\n        <i>\n         V\n        </i>\n        is electric potential or voltage in\n        <a href="/wiki/Volt" title="Volt">\n         volts\n        </a>\n       </dd>\n      </dl>\n      <p>\n       <a href="/wiki/Electricity_generation" title="Electricity generation">\n        Electricity generation\n       </a>\n       is often done with\n       <a href="/wiki/Electric_generator" title="Electric generator">\n        electric generators\n       </a>\n       , but can also be supplied by chemical sources such as\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electric_batteries" title="Electric batteries">\n        electric batteries\n       </a>\n       or by other means from a wide variety of sources of energy. Electric power is generally supplied to businesses and homes by the\n       <a href="/wiki/Electric_power_industry" title="Electric power industry">\n        electric power industry\n       </a>\n       . Electricity is usually sold by the\n       <a href="/wiki/Kilowatt_hour" title="Kilowatt hour">\n        kilowatt hour\n       </a>\n       (3.6 MJ) which is the product of power in kilowatts multiplied by running time in hours. Electric utilities measure power using\n       <a href="/wiki/Electricity_meter" title="Electricity meter">\n        electricity meters\n       </a>\n       , which keep a running total of the electric energy delivered to a customer. Unlike fossil fuels, electricity is a low\n       <a href="/wiki/Entropy" title="Entropy">\n        entropy\n       </a>\n       form of energy and can be converted into motion or many other forms of energy with high efficiency.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">\n        <a href="#cite_note-53">\n         [53]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Electronics">\n        Electronics\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       Main article:\n       <a href="/wiki/Electronics" title="Electronics">\n        electronics\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Arduino_ftdi_chip-1.jpg">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1920" data-file-width="2560" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Arduino_ftdi_chip-1.jpg/220px-Arduino_ftdi_chip-1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Arduino_ftdi_chip-1.jpg/330px-Arduino_ftdi_chip-1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Arduino_ftdi_chip-1.jpg/440px-Arduino_ftdi_chip-1.jpg 2x" width="220"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Arduino_ftdi_chip-1.jpg" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         <a href="/wiki/Surface-mount_technology" title="Surface-mount technology">\n          Surface mount\n         </a>\n         electronic components\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       Electronics deals with\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_circuit" title="Electrical circuit">\n        electrical circuits\n       </a>\n       that involve\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Active_component" title="Active component">\n        active electrical components\n       </a>\n       such as\n       <a href="/wiki/Vacuum_tube" title="Vacuum tube">\n        vacuum tubes\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Transistor" title="Transistor">\n        transistors\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Diode" title="Diode">\n        diodes\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Optoelectronics" title="Optoelectronics">\n        optoelectronics\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Sensor" title="Sensor">\n        sensors\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/Integrated_circuit" title="Integrated circuit">\n        integrated circuits\n       </a>\n       , and associated passive interconnection technologies. The\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nonlinear" title="Nonlinear">\n        nonlinear\n       </a>\n       behaviour of active components and their ability to control electron flows makes amplification of weak signals possible and electronics is widely used in\n       <a href="/wiki/Information_processing" title="Information processing">\n        information processing\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Telecommunications" title="Telecommunications">\n        telecommunications\n       </a>\n       , and\n       <a href="/wiki/Signal_processing" title="Signal processing">\n        signal processing\n       </a>\n       . The ability of electronic devices to act as\n       <a href="/wiki/Switch" title="Switch">\n        switches\n       </a>\n       makes digital information processing possible. Interconnection technologies such as\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Circuit_board" title="Circuit board">\n        circuit boards\n       </a>\n       , electronics packaging technology, and other varied forms of communication infrastructure complete circuit functionality and transform the mixed components into a regular working\n       <a href="/wiki/System" title="System">\n        system\n       </a>\n       .\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Today, most electronic devices use\n       <a href="/wiki/Semiconductor" title="Semiconductor">\n        semiconductor\n       </a>\n       components to perform electron control. The study of semiconductor devices and related technology is considered a branch of\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solid_state_physics" title="Solid state physics">\n        solid state physics\n       </a>\n       , whereas the design and construction of\n       <a href="/wiki/Electronic_circuit" title="Electronic circuit">\n        electronic circuits\n       </a>\n       to solve practical problems come under\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electronics_engineering" title="Electronics engineering">\n        electronics engineering\n       </a>\n       .\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Electromagnetic_wave">\n        Electromagnetic wave\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       Main article:\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_wave" title="Electromagnetic wave">\n        Electromagnetic wave\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       Faraday\'s and Amp\xc3\xa8re\'s work showed that a time-varying magnetic field acted as a source of an electric field, and a time-varying electric field was a source of a magnetic field. Thus, when either field is changing in time, then a field of the other is necessarily induced.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-uniphysics_21-8">\n        <a href="#cite_note-uniphysics-21">\n         [21]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">\n        :696\xe2\x80\x93700\n       </sup>\n       Such a phenomenon has the properties of a\n       <a href="/wiki/Wave" title="Wave">\n        wave\n       </a>\n       , and is naturally referred to as an\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_wave" title="Electromagnetic wave">\n        electromagnetic wave\n       </a>\n       . Electromagnetic waves were analysed theoretically by\n       <a href="/wiki/James_Clerk_Maxwell" title="James Clerk Maxwell">\n        James Clerk Maxwell\n       </a>\n       in 1864. Maxwell developed a set of equations that could unambiguously describe the interrelationship between electric field, magnetic field, electric charge, and electric current. He could moreover prove that such a wave would necessarily travel at the\n       <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_light" title="Speed of light">\n        speed of light\n       </a>\n       , and thus light itself was a form of electromagnetic radiation.\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maxwell%27s_Laws" title="Maxwell\'s Laws">\n        Maxwell\'s Laws\n       </a>\n       , which unify light, fields, and charge are one of the great milestones of theoretical physics.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-uniphysics_21-9">\n        <a href="#cite_note-uniphysics-21">\n         [21]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">\n        :696\xe2\x80\x93700\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Thus, the work of many researchers enabled the use of electronics to convert signals into\n       <a href="/wiki/Radio_frequency" title="Radio frequency">\n        high frequency\n       </a>\n       oscillating currents, and via suitably shaped conductors, electricity permits the transmission and reception of these signals via radio waves over very long distances.\n      </p>\n      <h2>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Production_and_uses">\n        Production and uses\n       </span>\n      </h2>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Generation_and_transmission">\n        Generation and transmission\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       Main article:\n       <a href="/wiki/Electricity_generation" title="Electricity generation">\n        Electricity generation\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       See also:\n       <a href="/wiki/Electric_power_transmission" title="Electric power transmission">\n        Electric power transmission\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/Mains_electricity" title="Mains electricity">\n        Mains electricity\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gorskii_04414u.jpg">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3010" data-file-width="3329" height="271" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Gorskii_04414u.jpg/300px-Gorskii_04414u.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Gorskii_04414u.jpg/450px-Gorskii_04414u.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Gorskii_04414u.jpg/600px-Gorskii_04414u.jpg 2x" width="300"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gorskii_04414u.jpg" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         Early 20th-century alternator made in\n         <a href="/wiki/Budapest" title="Budapest">\n          Budapest\n         </a>\n         ,\n         <a href="/wiki/Hungary" title="Hungary">\n          Hungary\n         </a>\n         , in the power generating hall of a\n         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydroelectric" title="Hydroelectric">\n          hydroelectric\n         </a>\n         station (photograph by\n         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prokudin-Gorsky" title="Prokudin-Gorsky">\n          Prokudin-Gorsky\n         </a>\n         , 1905\xe2\x80\x931915).\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       In the 6th century BC, the Greek philosopher\n       <a href="/wiki/Thales_of_Miletus" title="Thales of Miletus">\n        Thales of Miletus\n       </a>\n       experimented with amber rods and these experiments were the first studies into the production of electrical energy. While this method, now known as the\n       <a href="/wiki/Triboelectric_effect" title="Triboelectric effect">\n        triboelectric effect\n       </a>\n       , can lift light objects and generate sparks, it is extremely inefficient.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-batteries_54-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-batteries-54">\n         [54]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       It was not until the invention of the voltaic pile in the eighteenth century that a viable source of electricity became available. The voltaic pile, and its modern descendant, the\n       <a href="/wiki/Battery_(electricity)" title="Battery (electricity)">\n        electrical battery\n       </a>\n       , store energy chemically and make it available on demand in the form of electrical energy.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-batteries_54-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-batteries-54">\n         [54]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       The battery is a versatile and very common power source which is ideally suited to many applications, but its energy storage is finite, and once discharged it must be disposed of or recharged. For large electrical demands electrical energy must be generated and transmitted continuously over conductive transmission lines.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Electrical power is usually generated by electro-mechanical\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_generator" title="Electrical generator">\n        generators\n       </a>\n       driven by\n       <a href="/wiki/Steam" title="Steam">\n        steam\n       </a>\n       produced from\n       <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">\n        fossil fuel\n       </a>\n       combustion, or the heat released from nuclear reactions; or from other sources such as\n       <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_energy" title="Kinetic energy">\n        kinetic energy\n       </a>\n       extracted from wind or flowing water. The modern\n       <a href="/wiki/Steam_turbine" title="Steam turbine">\n        steam turbine\n       </a>\n       invented by\n       <a href="/wiki/Charles_Algernon_Parsons" title="Charles Algernon Parsons">\n        Sir Charles Parsons\n       </a>\n       in 1884 today generates about 80 percent of the\n       <a href="/wiki/Electric_power" title="Electric power">\n        electric power\n       </a>\n       in the world using a variety of heat sources. Such generators bear no resemblance to Faraday\'s homopolar disc generator of 1831, but they still rely on his electromagnetic principle that a conductor linking a changing magnetic field induces a potential difference across its ends.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">\n        <a href="#cite_note-55">\n         [55]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       The invention in the late nineteenth century of the\n       <a href="/wiki/Transformer" title="Transformer">\n        transformer\n       </a>\n       meant that electrical power could be transmitted more efficiently at a higher voltage but lower current. Efficient\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_transmission" title="Electrical transmission">\n        electrical transmission\n       </a>\n       meant in turn that electricity could be generated at centralised\n       <a href="/wiki/Power_station" title="Power station">\n        power stations\n       </a>\n       , where it benefited from\n       <a href="/wiki/Economies_of_scale" title="Economies of scale">\n        economies of scale\n       </a>\n       , and then be despatched relatively long distances to where it was needed.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Patterson_p44-48_56-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Patterson_p44-48-56">\n         [56]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">\n        <a href="#cite_note-57">\n         [57]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <div class="thumb tleft">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Parque_e%C3%B3lico_La_Muela.jpg">\n         <img alt="A wind farm of about a dozen three-bladed white wind turbines." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Parque_e%C3%B3lico_La_Muela.jpg/220px-Parque_e%C3%B3lico_La_Muela.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Parque_e%C3%B3lico_La_Muela.jpg/330px-Parque_e%C3%B3lico_La_Muela.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Parque_e%C3%B3lico_La_Muela.jpg/440px-Parque_e%C3%B3lico_La_Muela.jpg 2x" width="220"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Parque_e%C3%B3lico_La_Muela.jpg" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         <a href="/wiki/Wind_power" title="Wind power">\n          Wind power\n         </a>\n         is of increasing importance in many countries\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       Since electrical energy cannot easily be stored in quantities large enough to meet demands on a national scale, at all times exactly as much must be produced as is required.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Patterson_p44-48_56-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Patterson_p44-48-56">\n         [56]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       This requires\n       <a href="/wiki/Electric_utility" title="Electric utility">\n        electricity utilities\n       </a>\n       to make careful predictions of their electrical loads, and maintain constant co-ordination with their power stations. A certain amount of generation must always be held in\n       <a href="/wiki/Operating_reserve" title="Operating reserve">\n        reserve\n       </a>\n       to cushion an electrical grid against inevitable disturbances and losses.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Demand for electricity grows with great rapidity as a nation modernises and its economy develops. The United States showed a 12% increase in demand during each year of the first three decades of the twentieth century,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">\n        <a href="#cite_note-58">\n         [58]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       a rate of growth that is now being experienced by emerging economies such as those of India or China.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">\n        <a href="#cite_note-59">\n         [59]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">\n        <a href="#cite_note-60">\n         [60]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Historically, the growth rate for electricity demand has outstripped that for other forms of energy.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NRC1986_61-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-NRC1986-61">\n         [61]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">\n        :16\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Environmental_concerns_with_electricity_generation" title="Environmental concerns with electricity generation">\n        Environmental concerns with electricity generation\n       </a>\n       have led to an increased focus on generation from\n       <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">\n        renewable sources\n       </a>\n       , in particular from\n       <a href="/wiki/Wind_power" title="Wind power">\n        wind\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/Hydropower" title="Hydropower">\n        hydropower\n       </a>\n       . While debate can be expected to continue over the environmental impact of different means of electricity production, its final form is relatively clean.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NRC1986_61-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-NRC1986-61">\n         [61]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">\n        :89\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">\n        Applications\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gluehlampe_01_KMJ.png">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1276" data-file-width="768" height="282" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Gluehlampe_01_KMJ.png/170px-Gluehlampe_01_KMJ.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Gluehlampe_01_KMJ.png/255px-Gluehlampe_01_KMJ.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Gluehlampe_01_KMJ.png/340px-Gluehlampe_01_KMJ.png 2x" width="170"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gluehlampe_01_KMJ.png" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         The\n         <a href="/wiki/Incandescent_light_bulb" title="Incandescent light bulb">\n          light bulb\n         </a>\n         , an early application of electricity, operates by\n         <a href="/wiki/Joule_heating" title="Joule heating">\n          Joule heating\n         </a>\n         : the passage of\n         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Current_(electricity)" title="Current (electricity)">\n          current\n         </a>\n         through\n         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_resistance" title="Electrical resistance">\n          resistance\n         </a>\n         generating heat\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       Electricity is a very convenient way to transfer energy, and it has been adapted to a huge, and growing, number of uses.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62">\n        <a href="#cite_note-62">\n         [62]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       The invention of a practical\n       <a href="/wiki/Incandescent_light_bulb" title="Incandescent light bulb">\n        incandescent light bulb\n       </a>\n       in the 1870s led to\n       <a href="/wiki/Lighting" title="Lighting">\n        lighting\n       </a>\n       becoming one of the first publicly available applications of electrical power. Although electrification brought with it its own dangers, replacing the naked flames of gas lighting greatly reduced fire hazards within homes and factories.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63">\n        <a href="#cite_note-63">\n         [63]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Public utilities were set up in many cities targeting the burgeoning market for electrical lighting. In the late 20th century and in modern times, the trend has started to flow in the direction of deregulation in the electrical power sector.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">\n        <a href="#cite_note-64">\n         [64]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The resistive\n       <a href="/wiki/Joule_heating" title="Joule heating">\n        Joule heating\n       </a>\n       effect employed in filament light bulbs also sees more direct use in\n       <a href="/wiki/Electric_heating" title="Electric heating">\n        electric heating\n       </a>\n       . While this is versatile and controllable, it can be seen as wasteful, since most electrical generation has already required the production of heat at a power station.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">\n        <a href="#cite_note-65">\n         [65]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       A number of countries, such as Denmark, have issued legislation restricting or banning the use of resistive electric heating in new buildings.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">\n        <a href="#cite_note-66">\n         [66]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Electricity is however still a highly practical energy source for heating and\n       <a href="/wiki/Refrigeration" title="Refrigeration">\n        refrigeration\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67">\n        <a href="#cite_note-67">\n         [67]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       with\n       <a href="/wiki/Air_conditioning" title="Air conditioning">\n        air conditioning\n       </a>\n       /\n       <a href="/wiki/Heat_pump" title="Heat pump">\n        heat pumps\n       </a>\n       representing a growing sector for electricity demand for heating and cooling, the effects of which electricity utilities are increasingly obliged to accommodate.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68">\n        <a href="#cite_note-68">\n         [68]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Electricity is used within\n       <a href="/wiki/Telecommunication" title="Telecommunication">\n        telecommunications\n       </a>\n       , and indeed the\n       <a href="/wiki/Electrical_telegraph" title="Electrical telegraph">\n        electrical telegraph\n       </a>\n       , demonstrated commercially in 1837 by\n       <a href="/wiki/William_Fothergill_Cooke" title="William Fothergill Cooke">\n        Cooke\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/Charles_Wheatstone" title="Charles Wheatstone">\n        Wheatstone\n       </a>\n       , was one of its earliest applications. With the construction of first\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_Transcontinental_Telegraph" title="First Transcontinental Telegraph">\n        intercontinental\n       </a>\n       , and then\n       <a href="/wiki/Transatlantic_telegraph_cable" title="Transatlantic telegraph cable">\n        transatlantic\n       </a>\n       , telegraph systems in the 1860s, electricity had enabled communications in minutes across the globe.\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Optical_fibre" title="Optical fibre">\n        Optical fibre\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/Communications_satellite" title="Communications satellite">\n        satellite communication\n       </a>\n       have taken a share of the market for communications systems, but electricity can be expected to remain an essential part of the process.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The effects of electromagnetism are most visibly employed in the\n       <a href="/wiki/Electric_motor" title="Electric motor">\n        electric motor\n       </a>\n       , which provides a clean and efficient means of motive power. A stationary motor such as a\n       <a href="/wiki/Winch" title="Winch">\n        winch\n       </a>\n       is easily provided with a supply of power, but a motor that moves with its application, such as an\n       <a href="/wiki/Electric_vehicle" title="Electric vehicle">\n        electric vehicle\n       </a>\n       , is obliged to either carry along a power source such as a battery, or to collect current from a sliding contact such as a\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pantograph_(rail)" title="Pantograph (rail)">\n        pantograph\n       </a>\n       .\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Electronic devices make use of the\n       <a href="/wiki/Transistor" title="Transistor">\n        transistor\n       </a>\n       , perhaps one of the most important inventions of the twentieth century,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69">\n        <a href="#cite_note-69">\n         [69]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       and a fundamental building block of all modern circuitry. A modern\n       <a href="/wiki/Integrated_circuit" title="Integrated circuit">\n        integrated circuit\n       </a>\n       may contain several billion miniaturised transistors in a region only a few centimetres square.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70">\n        <a href="#cite_note-70">\n         [70]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Electricity is also used to fuel public transportation, including electric buses and trains.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">\n        <a href="#cite_note-71">\n         [71]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h2>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Electricity_and_the_natural_world">\n        Electricity and the natural world\n       </span>\n      </h2>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Physiological_effects">\n        Physiological effects\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       Main article:\n       <a href="/wiki/Electric_shock" title="Electric shock">\n        Electric shock\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       A voltage applied to a human body causes an electric current through the tissues, and although the relationship is non-linear, the greater the voltage, the greater the current.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tleis_72-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-tleis-72">\n         [72]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       The threshold for perception varies with the supply frequency and with the path of the current, but is about 0.1\xc2\xa0mA to 1\xc2\xa0mA for mains-frequency electricity, though a current as low as a microamp can be detected as an\n       <a href="/wiki/Electrovibration" title="Electrovibration">\n        electrovibration\n       </a>\n       effect under certain conditions.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73">\n        <a href="#cite_note-73">\n         [73]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       If the current is sufficiently high, it will cause muscle contraction,\n       <a href="/wiki/Fibrillation" title="Fibrillation">\n        fibrillation\n       </a>\n       of the heart, and\n       <a href="/wiki/Burn" title="Burn">\n        tissue burns\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tleis_72-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-tleis-72">\n         [72]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       The lack of any visible sign that a conductor is electrified makes electricity a particular hazard. The pain caused by an electric shock can be intense, leading electricity at times to be employed as a method of\n       <a href="/wiki/Torture" title="Torture">\n        torture\n       </a>\n       . Death caused by an electric shock is referred to as\n       <a href="/wiki/Electric_shock" title="Electric shock">\n        electrocution\n       </a>\n       . Electrocution is still the means of\n       <a href="/wiki/Capital_punishment" title="Capital punishment">\n        judicial execution\n       </a>\n       in some jurisdictions, though its use has become rarer in recent times.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74">\n        <a href="#cite_note-74">\n         [74]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Electrical_phenomena_in_nature">\n        Electrical phenomena in nature\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Electric-eel2.jpg">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1728" data-file-width="2304" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Electric-eel2.jpg/220px-Electric-eel2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Electric-eel2.jpg/330px-Electric-eel2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Electric-eel2.jpg/440px-Electric-eel2.jpg 2x" width="220"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Electric-eel2.jpg" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         The electric eel,\n         <i>\n          Electrophorus electricus\n         </i>\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       Main article:\n       <a href="/wiki/Electrical_phenomena" title="Electrical phenomena">\n        Electrical phenomena\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       Electricity is not a human invention, and may be observed in several forms in nature, a prominent manifestation of which is\n       <a href="/wiki/Lightning" title="Lightning">\n        lightning\n       </a>\n       . Many interactions familiar at the macroscopic level, such as\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Touch" title="Touch">\n        touch\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Friction" title="Friction">\n        friction\n       </a>\n       or\n       <a href="/wiki/Chemical_bond" title="Chemical bond">\n        chemical bonding\n       </a>\n       , are due to interactions between electric fields on the atomic scale. The\n       <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_magnetic_field" title="Earth\'s magnetic field">\n        Earth\'s magnetic field\n       </a>\n       is thought to arise from a\n       <a href="/wiki/Dynamo_theory" title="Dynamo theory">\n        natural dynamo\n       </a>\n       of circulating currents in the planet\'s core.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75">\n        <a href="#cite_note-75">\n         [75]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Certain crystals, such as\n       <a href="/wiki/Quartz" title="Quartz">\n        quartz\n       </a>\n       , or even\n       <a href="/wiki/Sugar" title="Sugar">\n        sugar\n       </a>\n       , generate a potential difference across their faces when subjected to external pressure.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-crystallography_76-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-crystallography-76">\n         [76]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       This phenomenon is known as\n       <a href="/wiki/Piezoelectricity" title="Piezoelectricity">\n        piezoelectricity\n       </a>\n       , from the\n       <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">\n        Greek\n       </a>\n       <i>\n        piezein\n       </i>\n       (\xcf\x80\xce\xb9\xce\xad\xce\xb6\xce\xb5\xce\xb9\xce\xbd), meaning to press, and was discovered in 1880 by\n       <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Curie" title="Pierre Curie">\n        Pierre\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Curie" title="Jacques Curie">\n        Jacques Curie\n       </a>\n       . The effect is reciprocal, and when a piezoelectric material is subjected to an electric field, a small change in physical dimensions takes place.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-crystallography_76-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-crystallography-76">\n         [76]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Some organisms, such as\n       <a href="/wiki/Shark" title="Shark">\n        sharks\n       </a>\n       , are able to detect and respond to changes in electric fields, an ability known as\n       <a href="/wiki/Electroreception" title="Electroreception">\n        electroreception\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Biodynamics_77-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Biodynamics-77">\n         [77]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       while others, termed\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrogenic" title="Electrogenic">\n        electrogenic\n       </a>\n       , are able to generate voltages themselves to serve as a predatory or defensive weapon.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Electroreception_3-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Electroreception-3">\n         [3]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       The order\n       <a href="/wiki/Gymnotiformes" title="Gymnotiformes">\n        Gymnotiformes\n       </a>\n       , of which the best known example is the\n       <a href="/wiki/Electric_eel" title="Electric eel">\n        electric eel\n       </a>\n       , detect or stun their prey via high voltages generated from modified muscle cells called\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrocytes" title="Electrocytes">\n        electrocytes\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Electroreception_3-2">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Electroreception-3">\n         [3]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-morris_4-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-morris-4">\n         [4]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       All animals transmit information along their cell membranes with voltage pulses called\n       <a href="/wiki/Action_potential" title="Action potential">\n        action potentials\n       </a>\n       , whose functions include communication by the nervous system between\n       <a href="/wiki/Neuron" title="Neuron">\n        neurons\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/Muscle" title="Muscle">\n        muscles\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-neural_science_78-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-neural_science-78">\n         [78]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       An electric shock stimulates this system, and causes muscles to contract.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79">\n        <a href="#cite_note-79">\n         [79]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Action potentials are also responsible for coordinating activities in certain plants.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-neural_science_78-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-neural_science-78">\n         [78]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h2>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_perception">\n        Cultural perception\n       </span>\n      </h2>\n      <p>\n       In 1850,\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/William_Gladstone" title="William Gladstone">\n        William Gladstone\n       </a>\n       asked the scientist\n       <a href="/wiki/Michael_Faraday" title="Michael Faraday">\n        Michael Faraday\n       </a>\n       why electricity was valuable. Faraday answered, \xe2\x80\x9cOne day sir, you may tax it.\xe2\x80\x9d\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Conversation_80-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-The_Conversation-80">\n         [80]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       In the 19th and early 20th century, electricity was not part of the everyday life of many people, even in the industrialised\n       <a href="/wiki/Western_world" title="Western world">\n        Western world\n       </a>\n       . The\n       <a href="/wiki/Popular_culture" title="Popular culture">\n        popular culture\n       </a>\n       of the time accordingly often depicts it as a mysterious, quasi-magical force that can slay the living, revive the dead or otherwise bend the laws of nature.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Van_Riper_69_81-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Van_Riper_69-81">\n         [81]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       This attitude began with the 1771 experiments of\n       <a href="/wiki/Luigi_Galvani" title="Luigi Galvani">\n        Luigi Galvani\n       </a>\n       in which the legs of dead frogs were shown to twitch on application of\n       <a href="/wiki/Galvanism" title="Galvanism">\n        animal electricity\n       </a>\n       . "Revitalization" or resuscitation of apparently dead or drowned persons was reported in the medical literature shortly after Galvani\'s work. These results were known to\n       <a href="/wiki/Mary_Shelley" title="Mary Shelley">\n        Mary Shelley\n       </a>\n       when she authored\n       <i>\n        <a href="/wiki/Frankenstein" title="Frankenstein">\n         Frankenstein\n        </a>\n       </i>\n       (1819), although she does not name the method of revitalization of the monster. The revitalization of monsters with electricity later became a stock theme in horror films.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       As the public familiarity with electricity as the lifeblood of the\n       <a href="/wiki/Second_Industrial_Revolution" title="Second Industrial Revolution">\n        Second Industrial Revolution\n       </a>\n       grew, its wielders were more often cast in a positive light,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Van_Riper_71_82-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Van_Riper_71-82">\n         [82]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       such as the workers who "finger death at their gloves\' end as they piece and repiece the living wires" in\n       <a href="/wiki/Rudyard_Kipling" title="Rudyard Kipling">\n        Rudyard Kipling\n       </a>\n       \'s 1907 poem\n       <i>\n        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sons_of_Martha" title="Sons of Martha">\n         Sons of Martha\n        </a>\n       </i>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Van_Riper_71_82-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Van_Riper_71-82">\n         [82]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Electrically powered vehicles of every sort featured large in adventure stories such as those of\n       <a href="/wiki/Jules_Verne" title="Jules Verne">\n        Jules Verne\n       </a>\n       and the\n       <i>\n        <a href="/wiki/Tom_Swift" title="Tom Swift">\n         Tom Swift\n        </a>\n       </i>\n       books.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Van_Riper_71_82-2">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Van_Riper_71-82">\n         [82]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       The masters of electricity, whether fictional or real\xe2\x80\x94including scientists such as\n       <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Edison" title="Thomas Edison">\n        Thomas Edison\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Charles_Steinmetz" title="Charles Steinmetz">\n        Charles Steinmetz\n       </a>\n       or\n       <a href="/wiki/Nikola_Tesla" title="Nikola Tesla">\n        Nikola Tesla\n       </a>\n       \xe2\x80\x94were popularly conceived of as having wizard-like powers.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Van_Riper_71_82-3">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Van_Riper_71-82">\n         [82]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       With electricity ceasing to be a novelty and becoming a necessity of everyday life in the later half of the 20th century, it required particular attention by popular culture only when it\n       <i>\n        stops\n       </i>\n       flowing,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Van_Riper_71_82-4">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Van_Riper_71-82">\n         [82]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       an event that usually signals disaster.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Van_Riper_71_82-5">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Van_Riper_71-82">\n         [82]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       The people who\n       <i>\n        keep\n       </i>\n       it flowing, such as the nameless hero of\n       <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Webb" title="Jimmy Webb">\n        Jimmy Webb\n       </a>\n       \xe2\x80\x99s song "\n       <a href="/wiki/Wichita_Lineman" title="Wichita Lineman">\n        Wichita Lineman\n       </a>\n       " (1968),\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Van_Riper_71_82-6">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Van_Riper_71-82">\n         [82]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       are still often cast as heroic, wizard-like figures.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Van_Riper_71_82-7">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Van_Riper_71-82">\n         [82]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h2>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">\n        See also\n       </span>\n      </h2>\n      <div aria-label="Portals" class="noprint portal plainlist tright" role="navigation" style="margin:0.5em 0 0.5em 1em;border:solid #aaa 1px">\n </a>\n         </span>\n        </li>\n       </ul>\n      </div>\n      <ul>\n       <li>\n        <a href="/wiki/Amp%C3%A8re%27s_circuital_law" title="Amp\xc3\xa8re\'s circuital law">\n         Amp\xc3\xa8re\'s circuital law\n        </a>\n        , connects the direction of an electric current and its associated magnetic currents.\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        <a href="/wiki/Electric_potential_energy" title="Electric potential energy">\n         Electric potential energy\n        </a>\n        , the potential energy of a system of charges\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        <a href="/wiki/Electricity_market" title="Electricity market">\n         Electricity market\n        </a>\n        , the sale of electrical energy\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        <a href="/wiki/Hydraulic_analogy" title="Hydraulic analogy">\n         Hydraulic analogy\n        </a>\n        , an analogy between the flow of water and electric current\n       </li>\n      </ul>\n        </i>\n            </sup>\n           </a>\n          </span>\n          <span class="reference-text">\n           <cite class="citation" id="CITEREFBullock2005">\n            Bullock, Theodore
